B00004RF9B|4.0|\\"Forbidden Planet\\", created at MGM in 1956, was a high-budget high-concept science fiction film among the many low-budget sci-fi flicks made for drive-in theaters in that decade. It doesn't possess the intellectualism of \\"The Day the Earth Stood Still\\", nor does it hold up as well, but the two films have common themes. Cdr. John Adams (Leslie Nielsen) leads a mission to the planet Altair-4, where an expedition landed 20 years before and has not been heard from since. His ship is warned away by Dr. Edward Morbius (Walter Pidgeon), a survivor of the earlier mission. Morbius explains the violent fate met by his colleagues and urges the visitors to leave. Only Morbius and his comely daughter Altaira (Ann Francis) have survived and thrived. Now aware of the disaster, Adams must await instructions from Earth. But some invisible threat still lurks on the planet.<br /><br />It was probably the film's brightly colored Cinemscope, friendly robot Robby, and neat-o special effects that most impressed audiences when it was released, but I was struck by \\"Forbidden Planet\\"s obvious influence on subsequent science fiction films and television. The impact on Gene Roddenberry's \\"Star Trek\\" was substantial, right down to the costumes, the communicators, and, of course, the allegory -though Roddenberry was decidedly more optimistic. \\"Forbidden Planet\\" posits that technology and knowledge do not fundamentally change people. A species cannot grow out of its violent primitivism. A gloomy message for a big, colorful, sci-fi adventure. I don't know what to make of the acting style, which I found peculiarly wooden. But this is a must-see for science fiction fans, if only because it is so recognizable in retrospect.<br /><br />The DVD (Warner 2000): This disc is two-sided, with full screen on one side and widescreen on the other. The print is perfectly watchable but not clean. There are white specks. A theatrical trailer (4 min) is included. George Lucas seems to have taken some visual inspiration from this trailer, though the \\"Star Wars\\" saga has more in common with B-movies. Subtitles and captioning for the hearing impaired are available in English, French, and Spanish.ler, though the \\"Star Wars\\" saga has more in common with B-movies. 1849514003|4.0|Cocos2d for iPhone is a typical cookbook. That means you will find here lots of recipes useful during iPhone development. Book is well structured. Each chapter is devoted to different aspect of development. There are topics covering basic topics like simple graphics manipulation but also cover physics related ones. One remark here. Not all topics are strictly related to Cocos2d. It can be slightly misleading by tittle that implies Cocos2d library will be discussed in the book. For example JSON, XML and PLIST are covered by TouchJSON, GDataXML and Cocoa libraries respectively. The same refers to SQLite related topics. You could argue whether this kind of topics should be put into book devoted to Cocos2d - no matter what, tittle suggests it is strictly graphics related. On the other hand, maybe it can be useful as sooner or later you will face these issues during game development.<br /><br />What I liked about the book? The structure of the recipes. They are nearly identical and you always know what to expect. At first, you get the description of the problem, how to prepare the environment, and eventually how to code the solution. There is also an explanation of how and why everything works. From time to time you will also find \\"there is more\\" section that points to valuable resources.<br /><br />What I didn't like? I think there are too much source code in the book. I know it is easier to read when you have the code in front of you, but I am pretty sure that most of the people will use the book together with their laptops and XCode running aside. So, in my opinion, basing on example source codes would be just fine. And the book would occupy less place on the book shelf.<br /><br />Anyway, I think it is worth considering to by this book (at least e-book format) just to have some nice recipes by you hand. Not all of them are applicable in each case, but still, they cover quite a loot of topics.

0143119419|5.0|I really liked this book because it did such a nice job of telling the history of hedge funds in story form (it reminded me of Lewis, The Big Short, Liars Poker, etc). The author did *extensive* research and many interviews with the players, so the book has the ring of truth, for sure. I had to (and wanted to) read it through twice because there was so much good material.  I particularly liked the attention the author paid to the reasons why hedge funds blew up, or were hurt badly -- typically, they got hurt bad when their trades were big, when they were leveraged too much, and when they couldn't get out of crowded trades when liquidity dried up and others started trading against the dying fund.  It was also very interesting to read the author's take on whether it makes sense to regulate hedge funds more. If anything, I went into the book thinking like most laymen, that hedge funds were somewhat destabilizing and unsavory players in the market, but I came out of the book with more understanding of why hedge funds can play a positive role in the market, and why it doesn't make much sense to attempt to regulate them even more.  An excellent, educational, and thought-provoking book, IMHO.

0307949508|2.0|Historic moments in fiction: Blomqvist refuses coffee! Fact! It happens on Page 349 of my paperback Vintage Crime edition. All kidding aside, I'm actually not bothered by all the coffee drinking in the Millennium saga; it often makes me wish I had a fresh brewed cup in my own hand.  But I do have plenty of problems with this book as a whole.<br /><br />Mild spoilers below.<br /><br />I had hopes for this installment, having been told the series gets better as it goes on. At first it seemed so. At least it didn't start with 100 pages on Swedish financial journalism. Lisbeth's new life was mildly intriguing. Back in Sweden, an actual mystery plot was introduced with the investigations into human trafficking and the double murder. And then it all got bogged down. Too many people doing the same investigation with very little to show for it. Endless repetition of facts. Pointless feuding among the police team. Or, to quote the book, \\"The three parallel investigations churned on.\\" At least twice, two people or groups arrived at the same conclusion at about the same time. Then there are innumerable characters with similar names speaking elliptically about current and past plots and events. It got so complicated that I gave up even trying to follow logically. Sometimes I wondered if the author did the same. It takes some doing to write about so many kidnappings, brutal beatings, torture, murders, hacking people with chain saws, and have it be a slow read. But so it is. Elements that should be shocking lose their impact under the sheer weight of extraneous material. I think Larsson could have dispensed with Milton Security and put the police investigation in the background, then highlighted Blomqvist and Salander separately homing in on the bad guys. The ultimate resolution of the plot is no great shakes, but could have been much more suspenseful if stripped down to essentials - and to characters we know.<br /><br />As many have commented, the characterization of Lisbeth is disappointing. In the first book, she was interesting, with special abilities but flawed and human. Here she is a cartoon superhero (though hero is perhaps the wrong word for someone so violently aggressive). She can do literally anything, boxing, breaking into any building, higher mathematics, you name it. A major error in my opinion was the way the relationship between Lisbeth and Blomqvist - one of the positives of Dragon Tattoo - was pushed to the background. Between the reimagining of Salander as a vigilante Mary Sue, and some wildly implausible plot twists, sometimes this reads like a bad fan-fic of the first book.<br /><br />I have to wonder at the publishing house that produced these books without much stricter editing. 1612130283|3.0|By now most of you have heard about the hoopla surrounding the very popular Fifty Shades trilogy by E.L. James. Fifty Shades of Grey has an astounding 4,300 reviews on Goodreads and all three books are currently the top three best selling digital books on Amazon for erotica. There's also quite some controversy surrounding E.L., this series and how it was published. From what I understand, Fifty Shades of Grey was originally titled, Master of the Universe and was Twilight fan fiction. The Twilight fan fic readers became obsessed with this book (and what's now the second and third book, Fifty Shades Darker and Fifty Shades Freed) that E.L. decided to publish it, or Writer's House Coffee Shop Publishing approached E.L. to publish it with them. Fifty Shades of Grey has sold more than 30,000 copies sold since May 2011 (at $10 a pop for an ebook, this confounds me).<br /><br />The phenomenon surrounding this trilogy is both incredible and scary. I can promise you because of the popularity of the Fifty Shades trilogy, you'll see more of these stories blatantly based on fan fiction being published in the coming years that will most likely sell well. Writers publishing fan fiction as original fiction isn't something new. Take a look at all the sequels and sometimes annoying retelling's of Pride and Prejudice as an example. But that's allowed because Pride and Prejudice is a free domain book. Writing and selling fan fiction based on Stephenie Meyer's Twilight series isn't free domain and it gets sketchy because of the issue of copyright. But I have to hand it to E.L. Because of the obsessive nature of Twilight fans, she took her story to a platform, knowing if she invoked Meyer's characters and inserted them in her own story (even if she used the names like Edward, Jacob, Bella and so forth) regardless if her own characters were no where near Meyer's characters in terms of personality or character traits, she'd have a hit on her hand.<br /><br />It's been awhile since a series made me roll my eyes and felt such raging annoyance over the actions of two characters. The last time I felt this way was while I read Karen Marie Moning's Fever series and the character of Jericho \\"Le Douche\\" Barrons. James's Christian Grey isn't a Barrons (thank God), and very early on I couldn't help but compare Christian to Roarke from JD Robb's In Death series. Christian is a disturbed megalomaniac, while Roarke doesn't need any form of therapy and  is everything a hero and man should be that women should proudly admit they're attracted to.<br /><br />Since Fifty Shades of Grey is based on Twilight, I really thought it would be exactly like Twilight, sans the paranormal element. Other than Ana accepting Christian and all his faults, much like Bella does with Edward, and the Pacific Northeast setting, I can't see any other comparisons between Fifty and Twilight. Fifty Shades of Grey is a Harlequin Presents on over the top crack that combines the characters and plot of the 1986's cult classic soft porn- 9  Weeks. The only way he can find sexual gratification is through BDSM or types of kinkery f'ery as he calls it. Christian fully admits he's fifty shades of f'ed up. But even though Christian is emotionally and mentally unstable (he doesn't like to be touched) he goes on to do great things. By the age of twenty-seven he's extremely rich, powerful and runs his own company. Women fall at his feet, but because he's never had a long term relationship with a woman, his family, who loves and adores him (his parents and a younger brother and sister) assumes he's gay.<br /><br />When Fifty Shades of Grey begins we meet Anastasia Steele, a soon to be college graduate who hopes to get a job in publishing. B004LWZWBU|1.0|You'd think a comedy starring Steve Martin, Owen Wilson, and Jack Black would be pretty funny, if not uproariously hilarious, especially when the film is centered around competitive bird watching. Based on the book by Mark Obmascik, The Big Year actually hopes to accomplish a bit more depth, but it largely falls flat. At various crossroads in their lives, Stu, Brad, and Kenny embark on a cross-country trip in hopes of spotting the most bird species in North America; along the way, they each discover enough about themselves to move on. 1451627289|5.0|\\"The past does not want to be changed\\"........This is the premise that drives 11-22-63, although this story is as much about nostalgia and the human experience as it is a very fresh and enjoyable take on time travel.<br /><br />Jake Epping a divorced 35 year old English teacher from 2011 is living a rather lonely life, as his ex wife has recently left him for someone she met in an AA meeting. Jake has few friends but he has befriended Al, the owner of a local diner who is able to sell his \\"real beef\\" burgers at suspiciously rock bottom prices. One day Al calls Jake to come over to the diner after hours to \\"show him something\\". Jake comes over only to find that Al who was healthy the day before, is in the last few days of terminal lung cancer and seems to have aged overnight. With little explanation as to his current state of health, Al decides showing Jake his secret is easier than telling him, and so Jake steps into the pantry of the diner, and into 1958....<br />I will not go into any more plot details than this, because \\"living\\" them along with Jake is what makes this a great read. King explores the terrible and sometimes catastrophic repercussions of trying to change the future by altering the past, and in this novelization you experience right along with our protagonist Jake that trying to alter seemingly unalterable events, (that changed lives and shaped history) is like wading though jello pudding, and is resistant to change. We also observe the horrors and ripple effects that these alterations bring about, the past, shall we say, will have it's day. At the end this novel asks the question; Was the past better left untouched? Even if you can prevent horrific events, was the world still a better place than the skewed reality created by changing them?<br /><br />Jake himself grows as a character, and in his five year journey he finds inner strengths that were deeply buried in the meek English teacher from 2011, and he even dares to love again. 11-22-63 also explores great questions of morality; selfish desires versus noble intentions, and whether the life of a friend or loved one is a sacrifice worth making for the life of a world leader.<br /><br />For me this was truly a page turner, and although a lot of parts of the book were a bit of a meander through the past, I never felt that it dragged because I experienced  life during 1958-1963 as if I had lived back then myself, so clear is the complete immersive experience that King gives us, the visuals... the smells...the sounds.... I also appreciated that King pulled no punches; as much as a beautiful sense of nostalgia that this novel gives you, you also experience the mindset of the day; A different restroom for those of another color, fear of communism, women truly being the lesser sex in society... I came away with a greater appreciation of both the past and the present. Of course like any King novel there is a little touch of the other-worldly going on here that we discover at the very end ;)<br /><br />Like any good book I was on a race to the finish, hoping I would never find it.morality; selfish desires versus noble intentions, and whether the life of a friend or loved one is a sacrifice worth making for the life of a world leader. 0812992792|5.0|This novel's masterful craftsmanship and subject matter bring to mind a comparison with [[ASIN:0451228146 One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich]]. In North Korea under Kim Jung Il (at least as Adam Johnson tells it here), the same is not true. This book is a testament to the human spirit, in that it is the story of how a man trains himself to keep such stories out of his head, understand the darkness for what it is, and learn to fight for dignity within that darkness.

0316069884|4.0|The impetus for Jonathan Safran Foer's Eating Animals, a monograph that details his reasoning for not eating animals, was the birth of his first child.  Foer wanted to be very sure that meat eating was as wrong as he thought it was, before he raised his son as vegetarian.  Eventually, Foer had amassed so much information on the topic, that he felt compelled to share it with others in the form of a book.  Foer's central thesis is that the divide between animals we eat, and animals that we do not eat, is arbitrary; meat-eating leads to extreme environmental degradation; and, most importantly, causes extreme suffering of almost every animal raised for food.   Prior to discussing the findings of his research, Foer briefly discusses the arbitrary divide between animals we eat, and animals that we do not.  For instance, Foer argues that there is no material reason why eating pigs, is any more justified than eating dogs.  In terms of intelligence and temperament, dogs differ very little from pigs and many others animals that American society deems appropriate for human consumption.  The reason, according to Foer, is that dogs have become companions, while other animals, such as pigs, cows, and chickens have not.  Foer puts it this way, \\"We care most about what's close to us, and have a remarkably easy time forgetting everything else.\\"  This arbitrary divide is found across cultures and there little uniformity to the animals that are considered friends or food.  Some cultures that eat dogs do not eat chickens, and some cultures that eat chickens do not eat dogs.   At this point in the text, Foer begins examining the devastating environmental effects of the twentieth-century innovation, known as factory farming.  Essentially, this method of farming is intended to maximize profits and meat production, while seriously decreasing overhead.  This accomplished by genetic manipulation, restricting mobility, and dramatic overcrowding of animals in indoor facilities.  While there issome variation in factory farming depending on the animal concerned, these facilities wreak unmitigated havoc on the environment.  The United Nation has even found that \\"farmed animals contribute more to climate change than transport.\\"  So, to put this in perspective, farmed animals contribute more to global warming than all of the cars, ships, trucks, airplanes, motorcycles, and tractors in the entire world.  Unfortunatley, the ozone is not the only thing polluted by factory farms.  For instance, the excrement produced by factory farms in the United States, which is estimated to be 130 times more than the human population produces, is dumped directly into open pits.  Over time, this waste leaks into nearby rivers, lakes, and oceans, and has been linked to numerous health problems in humans, including asthma, \\"persistent nosebleeds, earaches, chronic diarrhea, and burning lungs.\\"  Most surprisingly, considering that many so-called vegetarians eat fish, commercial fishing might even be more environmentally unfriendly.  Foer writes, for every pound of shrimp \\"26 pounds of other sea animals [are] killed and tossed back into the ocean.\\"   Most importantly, the animals that are used for food in the United States, whether they be pigs, cows, sheep, or fish, suffer horribly.  In one extreme case, investigators recorded slaughterhouse employees \\"administering daily beatings, bludgeoning pregnant sows with a wrench, and ramming an iron pole a foot deep into a mother pigs' rectums and vaginas.\\"  While this might be an extreme case, the regularly accepted treatment of food animals is not much better.  Chickens used for meat, known as \\"broilers,\\" are kept in \\"battery cages,\\" which wire cages \\"stacked between three and nine tiers high,\\" where each individual animals gets less than a square foot of space to move.  Chickens used for laying eggs, known as \\"layer chickens,\\" do not fair much better.  In fact, since male do not lay eggs they are destroyed by \\"being sucked through a series of pipes onto an electrified plate,\\" \\"tossed into large plastic containers\\" to starve or be suffocated, or \\"sent fully conscious through macerators,\\" which Foer likens to wood chippers.  Factory farmed cows, sheep, and fish suffer similarly.   Throughout this text, Foer persuasively supports his central thesis.  After even a quick review of the facts, the line between animals that we eat and do not eat, seems entirely arbitrary.  Even worse, due to the advent of factory farming, the environment is rapidly being destroyed and the suffering of animals is increasing to unparalleled levels.  What is yet to be seen, is how humans will deal with this dilemma during the twenty-first century.  Hopefully, works like Eating Animals will convince humankind that detriments of eating meat, far outweigh the benefits.  Bibliography  Foer, Jonathan Safran. Eating Animals. New York: Little, Brown and Co, 2009.

0062082353|4.0|On the surface, some may see Kyle's book as a great war story--it is that. But a deeper read of the book shows this to be a highly personalized account of the evolution of a man over the back-drop of war.  Overlayed onto the story of Mr. Kyle's war time experiences is the story of Chris's evolution as a man, as a soldier, but most of all as a father and husband. Woven in throughout the book is story of his courtship and marriage to Taya, and to their starting a family. The book shows the not so pleasant realities of the difficulty imposed on our military families by fathers and husbands answering the call of duty. I admire Kyle's candor about his putting country (and the SEALs) ahead of his family. Some authors would not be so willing to display their personal pocks like this.  I was afraid the story of his marital relationship and its difficulties would take away from the narrative, but far from doing so it enriched it especially with the commentary written by his wife which added dimension to the personal drama of Kyle's 4 deployments. One has to appreciate a man whose patriotism and sense of duty called him away from home and family so many times. Some might condemn his priorities. But without such men, who would fight our battles?  As mentioned, the book shows Mr. Kyle's evolution away from a priority list of God, country and family and turning it into the more correct sequence of God, family country. At the end of 4 long, eventful deployments, Mr. Kyle clearly came to realize what's most important in life and that a man should only be asked to sacrifice so much for his nation--and that his family needed him more than the war did. You have to give Chris credit for this awakening. Not that what he did in taking 4 deployments was wrong--far from it--I believe he was doing what God intended for his life. But he did the right thing leaving the military when he did. Clearly it was time.  The events he was involved in during the Iraq War are of course the main focus of the book and for me the most engrossing. Chris is a great story teller and the battles he fought are well drawn and really pull you in. The Fallujah chapters were particularly interesting. I felt like I was there in the thick of the battle with Chris and his team. The story of the 2400 yard kill he made near the end of his final deployment was truly amazing. Mr. Kyle's wicked sense of humor also shines through the narrative as well. This is anything but a dry read.  Not as big a page turner as his buddy Marcus Lutrell's book \\"Lone Survivor,\\" \\"American Sniper\\" nonetheless deserves its own honored place in the realms of recent war literature. While \\"Lone Survivor's\\" chapters on Seal training I felt were stronger, Kyle's book nonethless does a nice job describing this grueling process. This book is certainly more personal than Lutrell's book, and I think I came away with a better sense of who Chris Kyle is than I did from Lutrell and his book.  My only quibbles with the book are I wish he had used less profanity. I know that's how soldiers talk and I don't want to rob the book of Chris's personality (which comes out abundantly in the volume). I just thought it was a bit excessive.  I would have been interested too in having more dates to the events described to give us a better idea of the time line of Chris's tale. I think the book could have used a little more disciplined editing too, eliminating some of the one and two sentence paragraphs and the sections describing the guns Chris used which didn't really interest me not being much of a gun guy. But I'm sure this will be of great interest to gun enthusiasts. I just thought that aspect could have been blended into the war stories. Despite that, the book is well-paced and never bogs down at any point other than perhaps the gun section, but even then just barely. But these are minor critiques.  To the left wing scolds reviewing the book on here who want to dismiss Chris as a \\"warmonger,\\" stop playing so predictably to type with your tiresome self-righteousness. I have my doubts you even read the book. Chris isn't some blood thirsty fiend. He was a man who had a job to do and did it well and took justifiable pride in it--even if you don't understand that or agree with the Iraq War. He truly felt what he was doing made the world a better, safer place. Instead of simpering over people who'd gladly kill you and your family merely for being an American try thinking of the lives he saved, both American and Iraqi, by taking out the terrorist beasts who wanted to use Iraq to impose their nihilistic, oppressive and monsterous perversion of Islam on Iraq and the region. Who are those of you who never served and were never in Iraq to question Chris's motives?  Overall a book that will draw you in and hold you. Well told, unafraid, candid, honest, unvarnished. Admirable traits for a war biography in such PC times as ours that discourage the idea of there being any glory to be had in war. Mr. Kyle does a competent job putting the lid on that sentiment. Thank you Mr. Kyle for all you did for me and for all you did for all of us.

Jason Momoa tries, but he's really not given much terms of dialogue. Schwarzenegger didn't have a ton to say in the 1982 movie, but I think he had better dialogue than Momoa's Conan does. Not only that, but Arnold's Conan had a ton more personality than Momoa's version. Stephen Lang has the best role as the villain Zym. In fact, he's about the only character in the whole movie that has any life to him whatsoever. There's lots of blood and some nudity, but it doesn't mean anything because the story is so lackluster and the characters are so lifeless.<br /><br />The movie isn't a complete waste, but it could have been so much more. In short, if you are a fan of Howard's novels or the character of Conan, you will probably want to watch the movie. Action fans will probably be split on whether they like it or not. Cinder strays far enough from its premise as a retelling to be engaging, but yet again, a YA novel hinges on the relationship between the protagonist and the male love interest. The world building had far more cohesion and stuff going on that in most pseudo science fictional YA settings, which again, was somewhat interesting.  I was also slightly disappointed with the fact that it is the first installment in a series, but again, its YA, what am I expecting? Overall, a quick, rather engaging read, even though my first reaction on finishing it was to pick up \\"The Diamond Age,\\" because I was curious what effect nearly 15 years would have on the kind of technology portrayed in a superficially similar world would be.  The answer? Not that much-- though Diamond Age actually bothers hypothesizing how such tech would work--radio antennas having to be a certain size! How quaint! But yes, Cinder is very clearly a lay person writing science fiction novel, which isn't necessarily a bad thing....except when you can directly compare it to one that, well, isn't. Still a fun read, but lacking

0812992792|5.0|This isn't a story, a narrative, a movie concept. It is a novel in the truest sense -- an examination of the human condition through what to most readers, including myself, is a bizarre but ultimately utterly believable mileu -- the Democratic People's Republic of Korea in the near past, under Kim Jong Il.  I believe this should become a classic novel, a hope made possible by the timely demise of the real-life Kim Jong Il. had he lived to be overthrown by a military coup, or had gone through some kind of transformation like Qaddafi with his weapons programs, the \\"Kim Jong Il\\" of this story would be exposed as unreal. As it is, we'll never know, so this novel can live forever.  I do not know to what degree the author's depiction of the DPRK is accurate -- I certainly pray that it is an extraordinary exaggeration. But the author's skill is such that you can believe in it, exaggerated or not.  Imagine \\"1984\\", but not as mere speculation, but rather as current description -- made all the more believable through the portrayal of the clear ineptitude of the regime in so many respects other than in keeping its iron grip on power and on its people.  Read it. It will remain with you.

B0064NTZJO|5.0|A ton of love and hate for this film, but I went in undeterred to see if Ryan Gosling could finally be in a worthy Blu Ray - and I discovered he found his way onto a great product preservation here.<br /><br />The story follows the \\"driver with a code\\" formula who falls in love with a lady and does things to mess with the code (actually that summary is virtually taken from the producers of the film, they themselves admitting there was nothing different about this film - except for how it was filmed). The Blu quality is amazing, and depending on the scene it will be testing your blacks and dark motion sensitivity to the extreme. There are two LA skyline night shots that are worthy of pausing and looking for detail clarity. Several other shots with different cameras are almost Michael Mann-ish in their grain and coloring, but so is a lot of this film. The sound is a mixed bag - beautiful yet possibly dangerous depending how high you might be cranking it up to hear the center channel subtleties laced for 15 minutes - followed unexpectedly with a flood of high pitches/decibel increase (follow the hints with the music - when it starts to fade or the lights are coming back to brightness after fading - quick turn it back down before your center gets blown). The DTS is great in the two main chase sequences and the motors were overamped just right.<br /><br />The special features are definitely a mixed bag:<br /><br />* Cut to the Chase, 4:35 minutes. Listed it first because this was the one I had the most interest in...specifically how they pulled off that reverse 270 with the actress in the shot. Unfortunately this was way too short and only glossed over what should have been a 30 minute piece on the stunts...worthy enough though to discover that sequence was not CGI.<br />* Driver & Irene, 6:14 minutes. This and the next two are so clip-heavy they provide so little substance - all forgettable. This one follows the love story angle with talking heads and clips.<br />* Under the Hood, 11:50 minutes. Not what I had hoped as I was looking for car and motor info. Instead it is an extremely clip-heavy talking head piece about the actors and their takes on characters.<br />* I Drive, 5:26 minutes. A confused quip that looks like it was cut from the the prior segment about actors working with the director.<br />* Drive without the Driver, 25:41 minutes. Wow, I cannot think of a more boring way to get all of the information that leads up to the film being made, how it was cast, and so forth. The director (who I respect from his prior work - he assuredly has an eye for filming) sits on a hood of a car and rambles for 25 minutes about the process, essentially a commentary - but as an interview. If you are fans of commentaries (which I am) then you will dig this segment - otherwise a skipper.<br /><br />Overall a fascinating look at a performance film preserved on a solid Blu that has a great picture quality and test-worthy sound, the supplements ding this from being a five but there are no half-stars here...4 for the film and BD specs, .5 star for the supplements.had hoped as I was looking for car and motor info. B000HL093A|5.0|I should warn the reader that I am a grad student in philosophy so my review drifts off into fairly lengthy discussions of some of the philosophical issues raised by this film. If that kind of thing does not interest you I would suggest skipping my review.<br /><br />This is, apparently, one of the most controversial movies ever made. That, on its own, does not necessarily make it a good movie but the movie does possess one quality that I think all genuinely good movies should possess, and that is memorability. I do not actually watch very many movies but sometimes even I have trouble remembering what happened in movies I know I have seen. Hollywood churns out so many movies and so many of them are just not memorable at all. Whether you love it or hate it there is no doubt that Natural Born Killers is memorable.<br /><br />I just re-watched Natural Born Killers, it had been years since I had last seen it, but there were scenes that I still remembered and that were still very vivid in my mind even years later. The scene where Mallory's incestuous and abusive family life is portrayed as a sitcom, with a laugh track that is activated after her father's incestuous comments, is one of the most memorable scenes in all of cinema (interestingly Quentin Tarantino, who wrote the original script for Natural Born Killers which was then heavily re-written apparently, really detested that scene and walked out of the theater. I would be very curious to know what he detested so much about that scene since, to me, it is one of the most interesting scenes in the movie).<br /><br />Despite being over the top in many ways the movie still succeeds in presenting real characters with true emotions. The characters are full blooded and well drawn. Mickey and Mallory are mass murderers and the movie does not shy away from that. But despite being a killer Mickey is still sentimental and romantic and Mallory is still insecure and jealous. People are fascinated by evil so a lot of times in movies like this they purposely make the characters as inhuman and evil as possible. This movie chose to present mass murderers as full blooded, and believable, human beings and I suspect that is partly what led to some of the controversy surrounding the film.<br /><br />The movie also avoids what I think is the biggest problem I have with almost everything put out by Hollywood: false emotion. Almost everything Hollywood puts out is so full of sentimentality and false emotion. In most movies and television shows there will be a moment of \\"emotional truth\\" where the character finally comes to some self-realization but when they attempt to express their insight it always feels to me like they are reading right out of a psychology textbook. Everyone feels the way they think they are supposed to feel rather than how they actually do feel. I think there are probably people who live their life that way but it seems very difficult for movies to reach any kind of emotional truth. They almost always blow it, in my opinion, either by simply quoting the textbook or by attempting to over dramatize it. This movie avoids all of that. There are realizations in the movie but they ring true rather than false (like when Mickey and Mallory are with the Native American in his hut and Mickey says, \\"You feel the demons in here Mallory\\" and she says, \\"I think we're the demons\\").<br /><br />The movie is also cinematically brilliant. It is shot in a very unique way cutting between black and white and color, real life and cartoons, with strange projections often playing in the background. That is part of what makes the film memorable and it can be disconcerting at first. The first time I saw the movie I really did not know what to make of it but the second time I watched it I felt like it all made sense and worked really well. The cuts often allow us to see the \\"outer\\" event and the \\"inner\\" event. The best example being from the opening scene in the diner where the waitress says something, it cuts to black and white and she says it again but in a way where it becomes clear she is flirting with Mickey, the inner meaning of the event is portrayed, which then explains Mallory's rudeness to her (and the fact that she cheats in her einee-meanie minie-moe game).<br /><br />The movie also deals with a number of interesting themes. Of course in interviews Oliver Stone always highlights the theme of the media and the way in which the media portrays violence. While this is certainly a theme of the movie I do not think it is the most interesting theme and, like T.S. Eliot, I do not think that artists are always the best interpreters of their own work. Oliver Stone may have meant the movie to be an indictment of the media but I think the movie is more than that, and much more interesting.<br /><br />Art, in my opinion, is about much more than aesthetics, or, what is sensorially appealing. Art should aim (like almost all human endeavors) at self-knowledge. One could blame the media for the sensationalism of violence but I do not think that the media is really capable of force feeding us something that we do not already have an appetite for. The really interesting question is a question that few people want to ask: what is it about human beings that makes us crave violence? Why do we like watching television shows about mass murderers and serial killers? Why do we like watching natural disasters on television?<br /><br />Some reviewers criticized Stone for pointing his finger at the media while being unable or unwilling to point it at himself. That may or may not be true of Stone (I do not know the man) but it does not seem to me to be true about the movie. Anyone who watches this movie, enjoys it, and then criticizes the media without asking themselves why they like watching a movie about mass murderers turned into celebrities is lacking in self-reflection. That is the truly interesting question. But it is not a question that we like to ask.<br /><br />One of the defining moments in the movie for me is when they are interviewing the teenagers who are fans of Mickey and Mallory and one of them says, \\"Of course we do not condone murder\\" but you can see the light in his eyes as he says it and the fascination, and even admiration, he feels for Mickey and Mallory. He is not lying. He really does believe murder is wrong. After thousands of years of moral education we are all ready with the judgment \\"murder is wrong\\". And that is a good thing. But there is still that strange fascination with violence. It is like an ancient and unused altar of human sacrifice standing in the square of a modern, civilized city. We are civilized human beings now but there is still this relic of something ancient within us. It is an artifact that we cannot quite explain. It is, I think, similar to what Edgar Allen Poe called \\"the imp of the perverse\\".<br /><br />I recognize it in various forms. For example, I was watching a show recently on super volcanoes. There is apparently a super volcano underneath Yellowstone National Park which, if it were to erupt, could be catastrophic for life on earth, and as I was watching I noticed a strange phenomenon. On the one hand, I wanted the scientists to say something reassuring like, \\"it is not expected to erupt for millions of years\\", or \\"of course, even if it does erupt we will have some warning and it will not destroy all life as we know it\\". But there was also a part of me that wanted the opposite, and that wanted them to say, \\"we have no idea when it might erupt\\", and, \\"if it does erupt it will wipe out life as we know it\\". This, to me, is part of the same strange phenomenon that this movie is addressing. We hate and fear violence and we find it morally repulsive, and yet, we are fascinated by it.<br /><br />Natural Born Killers is a violent movie but I think the controversy has more to do with the fact that it holds up a mirror to ourselves. We want to identify with our moral self and banish that strange relic from ancient times, or project it onto others, but, as Mickey says during his interview, you cannot run from your own shadow. There are plenty of movies that are more violent than Natural Born Killers but I think part of what makes Natural Born Killers so shocking, and why it raised so much controversy, is because it implicates us in that violence.<br /><br />We love violence but we also like to keep a safe moral distance from that violence. That is partly why we are so fascinated with the holocaust. We can indulge our fascination with violence, we can peer into the heart of evil, and exercise our moral judgment at the same time. We do not want to admit our fascination with violence so we turn it into a moral lesson. What is shocking about Natural Born Killers is the way in which it forces us to look at the violence within ourselves. We do not get to project our own violence onto the Nazis or anyone else. We see our own fascination with violence and I think people find that disconcerting. I know I do. Like everyone else I like to identify with my better nature, the angel on my shoulder rather than the demon.<br /><br />But I also believe that it is necessary for us to see clearly the violence within ourselves if we are to overcome it. Otherwise we act it out unconsciously. We identify with our moral selves and project our shadows onto the Nazis or the serial killer on television while exercising subtle forms of cruelty within our own daily lives. The one character in the movie that recognizes the demons within Mickey and Mallory on first sight is the Native American character but he is also the only character who does not engage in hypocritical forms of moral judgment. The cop and the warden both consider themselves morally superior to Mickey and Mallory, the warden scoffs when Mickey says that all God's creatures commit murder, and yet they are both just as violent as Mickey and Mallory in their own ways. The Native American character, however, knows and recognizes the demon, i.e. he is conscious. And he is the only character that has any kind of positive effect on Mickey and Mallory and who is not simply a part of the chain of perpetuating violence.<br /><br />Ultimately I think this movie deserves the highest praise for raising all of these issues and dealing with them in a fairly profound and also entertaining way. I read that this movie was originally based on a story by Quentin Tarantino but that Oliver Stone and company made some major changes to the script. Quentin Tarantino was apparently not very happy about their changes. On the one hand, I can understand where Quentin Tarantino is coming from. I would not like it if someone totally rewrote something I had written either. On the other hand, I am glad that it wound up being an Oliver Stone movie rather than a Quentin Tarantino movie. I have a feeling that I would not have liked the Quentin Tarantino version nearly as much. I do not have anything against Quentin Tarantino, I do like some of his films, but I have never liked a Quentin Tarantino movie as much as I liked this film, and no Quentin Tarantino movie has ever inspired the kind of philosophical thoughts in me that this movie has inspired. While I can understand Tarantino's frustration, from the standpoint of art I think things worked out for the best...f Mickey and Mallory and one of them says, \\"Of course we do not condone murder\\" but you can see the light in his eyes as he says it and the fascination, and even admiration, he feels for Mickey and Mallory. And he is the only character that has any kind of positive effect on Mickey and Mallory and who is not simply a part of the chain of perpetuating violence.  Ultimately I think this movie deserves the highest praise for raising all of these issues and dealing with them in a fairly profound and also entertaining way. I read that this movie was originally based on a story by Quentin Tarantino but that Oliver Stone and company made some major changes to the script. Quentin Tarantino was apparently not very happy about their changes. On the one hand, I can understand where Quentin Tarantino is coming from. I would not like it if someone totally rewrote something I had written either. On the other hand, I am glad that it wound up being an Oliver Stone movie rather than a Quentin Tarantino movie. I have a feeling that I would not have liked the Quentin Tarantino version nearly as much. I do not have anything against Quentin Tarantino, I do like some of his films, but I have never liked a Quentin Tarantino movie as much as I liked this film, and no Quentin Tarantino movie has ever inspired the kind of philosophical thoughts in me that this movie has inspired. While I can understand Tarantino's frustration, from the standpoint of art I think things worked out for the best...

B005G0ETV0|5.0|28 January 2012 marks 125 years since Rubinstein was born. A great time to consider his legacy in this marvellous set.  The title words to this review are those Harvey Sachs uses in the liner notes to the original Rubinstein Collection to describe how those of us who were lucky enough to hear Rubinstein play live felt after one of his performances. As Sachs says, those words also describe how one feels after hearing Rubinstein's recording of Chopin's B Minor Sonata.  This set is full of such wonderful performances. The recording of the Busoni arrangement of Bach's Chaconne is titanic and must rank among the greatest piano recordings. It exemplifies what Eunice Podis (quoted by Sachs in his excellent biography of Rubinstein) says of Rubinstein's ability to shape very long legato lines, especially in Chopin. Barenboim, also quoted by Sachs refers to something unerring about Rubinstein's healthy musical intellect and Jeremy Siepmann in sleeve notes to the BBC Legends series speaks of his powerful, all embracing sense of rythm, stating that his playing was straightforward, natural simple and healthy. Siepmann also refers to Rubinstein's ability to hear and balance voices in single chords.  Then there is Rubinstein's natural and delicate use of rubato and his gloriously beautiful tone. I would add to the learned opinions above that Rubinstein's sheer love of the music he plays shines through time after time.  You will hear and be touched by these qualities countless times throughout this set. Listen to the delicate weaving of Chopin's Nocturnes, especially Op. 55, no.2 in the 1967 recording and the powerful sense of structure in his Ballades and Scherzos. The recording of the F minor Ballade is another towering performance.  The 1932 recording of Tchaikovsky's First Piano Concerto under Barbirolli is out of this world and the Andantino unbearably beautiful. This recording also demonstrates the amazing clarity of even the earliest recordings in this set thanks to the legendary Fred Gaisberg who produced the original recordings and to the painstaking transfers by Ward Marston.  Going to the very end of the set, despite some imperfections, Rubinstein's Decca recording of the Brahms D Minor Concerto with the Israel Philharmonic under Mehta is of such intensity and so full of love for the music that for me it is an essential reading of this great work. Rubinstein was 89 with only one more recording session to go and weeks away from his final ever recital at London's Wigmore Hall on  31 May 1976.  Unusually for such a prominent musician, Rubinstein recorded a lot a chamber music and there are many beautiful chamber recordings here. No less of an opposite in many ways to Rubinstein than Glenn Gould was quoted in Look magazine in 1971 as saying in an interview with Rubinstein that the Rubinstein/Guarneri Quartet recording of the Brahms Quintet was the greatest chamber music performance with piano he had heard in his life and that the recording had changed his view of what Brahms represented.  One of the pleasures of the set is a wonderful photograph of Rubinstein and Gould in the beatifully illustrated and well set out book which accompanies the set.  In addition there are lovely reproductions of the original LP sleeves and the CD's (with the original LP length programmes) are illustrated with the original labels and made to look like mini records. The earliest recordings are in covers cleverly representing 78's in plain sleeves showing only the record label. It is all a pleasure to look at and I find the original LP sleeve notes legible despite their reduced size.  I am still discovering new beauties in the original Rubinstein Collection after ten years and have returned to favourite recordings dozens of times.  Buy this incredible bargain set: 142 CD's (the equivalent of 95 full length CD's) and 2 DVD's for the price of about 20 CD's. It will reward you with a lifetime of pleasure.

B005QJZ5FA|4.0|On the internet, it's impossible to navigate through the waters of indie pop without encountering the firestorm over Lana Del Rey.  A surprising amount of consternation, anger, and love has been expressed by the masses over this fresh face in indie (and one of the biggest arguments is that she is NOT indie whatsoever).  Some also say that she's a terrible musician; everyone saw the disappointing SNL performance, so I won't even bother going into details about that.  The biggest argument out there is that she's \\"fake.\\"  Her lips, her name, her whole persona... it's all an invention.  Well, without buying into the hype, or anti-hype, rather, I prefer to judge music for the... well, music; not the blog-B.S. That surrounds it.  If it sounds good, it has my attention and I will listen to it.  It's as simple as that.<br /><br />The following is a song-by-song review of the album.  Some people appreciate this type of review and others do not.  If you fall into the latter camp, scroll to the bottom for my closing thoughts.<br /><br />Born to Die - The album opens with a breathy Lana, crooning over a Twin Peaks-esque guitar line tucked carefully behind layers of programmed strings.  A fitting introduction to the new world of Lana Del Rey (10/10)<br /><br />Off to the Races - This song is interesting because it introduces elements of hip-hop into Del Rey's sound.  When she raps and her vocals are doubled, it's reminiscent of the band Why?.  So far, so good!  (10/10)<br /><br />Blue Jeans - It becomes apparent at this point that we're probably going to hear that screamy, awkward loop throughout the album (some guy screaming \\"Lana?\\") and it gets a bit irritating.  Aside from that, the atmospheric strings and guitar help move things along.  (7/10)<br /><br />Video Games - This is the song that put Lana on the map, and it's easily the best on the album in every way: lyrically, melodically, production-wise, etc.  If you're new to the LDR hype, listen to this one first.  It's a good litmus test.  (10/10)<br /><br />Diet Mountain Dew - This song could easily transition to the radio with its chorus-centric formula.  It's also interesting that this song marks the third Mountain Dew inspired indie song (behind those penned by Daniel Johnston and Beck).  (7/10)<br /><br />National Anthem - We have reached the point in the album where things get a little shaky.  The lyrics are pretty bad here, guys.  Normally, LDR's lazier lyrics don't bother me but here they're practically thrown in your face.  \\"Money is the reason we exist, everybody knows it, it's a fact, kiss kiss.\\"  (4/10)<br /><br />Dark Paradise - That screaming guy buried in the mix makes his grand return (why was this thrown in there so often?) but the song itself is a decent ballad that gets a little danceable at certain points.  (6/10)<br /><br />Radio - Another album standout, Radio is instantly catchy.  The vocal melody is among the best of her repertoire.  Easy one of LDR's best.  (10/10)<br /><br />Carmen - Was this song written for the next James Bond film?  It definitely drives her style home, with its film noir vibes and smoky bar crooning.  It's called Carmen for god's sake.  What else do you need to know?  (6/10)<br /><br />Million Dollar Man - This song strikes me as an attempt to reproduce \\"Video Games.\\"  It's strikingly similar.  It's not bad, but it's not terribly original either.  (7/10)<br /><br />Summertime Sadness - The dramatic strings and bells at the beginning seem to suggest that this song takes place in early 1900's Mexico, moments before a duel.  Lyrically, we have another clunker.  The music is decent enough to keep your attention though, even though the chorus is similar to that of \\"National Anthem.\\"  (5/10)<br /><br />This Is What Makes Us Girls - A fitting closer, This Is What Makes Us Girls sums up the character of Lana Del Rey: it has the programmed strings, the screamy-guy loop (such a bad decision), the somewhat grating lyrics \\"Pabst Blue Ribbon on ice\\" (were her producers attempting to establish hipster credit with that line?), and the uniquely excellent voice.  Take it or leave it.  (9/10)<br /><br />Overall, LDR's debut is a bit uneven and somewhat repetitive but overall, quite catchy.  I would recommend that everyone set aside their preconceived notions of Lana as a blog entity and give this album a shot. B00000DSEK|5.0|Ten years after Ozzy's impressive debut with Randy Rhoads on Blizzard of Oz, Ozzy was at a crossroads both personally and professionally. After Jake E. Lee left the band at the end of The Ultimate Sin Tour in late 87' Ozzy was left hanging without his superstar sidekick. After many try-outs with various guitarists, Ozzy found Zakk Wylde, only 21 at the time, to fill Lee's big shoes. Zakk was already a well-respected guitar player when he was in his late teens. He jumped at the chance to help Ozzy continue his successful solo career. Wylde still refers to Ozzy as \\"The Boss\\" to this day, a reflection of Wylde's deep respect for Ozzy as a fine muscian. Their first album together, No Rest For The Wicked had moderate success and spawned the single \\"Crazy Babies\\". Undaunted, Ozzy returned three years after this album in 1991 with No More Tears. The album clearly displayed much promise, as it quickly went up the charts to become Ozzy's first and only number 1 album. Elevated by such gems as No More Tears, I Don't Want To Change The World, Time After Time, Mama I'm Coming Home, and the mature and experienced The Road To Nowhere, all of which had killer videos and dominated MTV rotation until Metallica's Black Album, Ozzy was a force to be reckoned with. Wylde's playing was inspired and vigorous, bassist Mike Inez(who later replaced Starr in Alice In Chains), threw down impressive chops, as well as Randy Castillo on drums. The band, even though Inez and Wylde were some 20 years Ozzy's junior, developed a great chemistry that was displayed throughout the albums entirety. Ferocious leads with stellar perfomances by Inez and Castillo, and some of Ozzy's best vocals, put this album into the classic category. This album is a must have for Ozzy fans and muscians alike.

He just stated - they had high inflation and hight growth rate - i.e. high inflation leads to high growth rate. Could it be true? - I think our world and economy is more complex and many factors should be taken into account, as well as these countries' economies should be compared to other similar ones that has quite close factors but lack of inflation.  Another example of author playing with factual base is about protectionism - he says that since US employed protectionists policies in previous centuries it is beneficial to employ them now and that free trade is bad since so many smart man in the past supported protectionism (quite questionable arguments and author isn't coming to conclusion by employing better arguments).  Or one more: he compares the US to China and India by stating that they achieve higher than US growth rate recently only because they do not employ free markets, forgetting that in both of these countries substantial growth began only when they liberated legislation (i.e. moved towards free markets). He also forgets or hides that grows in China and India highly were achieved because of cheap labor that leads to cheaper goods.  And there is much more and more examples of how author plays with the facts in the book. To conclude, author tries to prove his point of view by employing questionable arguments, that have nothing in common with scientific method and by doing so distort facts.  By the way, you won't find anything about capitalism about this book except continuing grumbling that it's bad and doesn't work.

145162445X|5.0|\\"Universe From Nothing\\" is a thought-provoking book, detailing how science has went about discovering the origins of the universe.  Krauss' subject matter is derived from the observable experiments and Data supporting the theory that the universe was created due to the big bang, which itself arose out of a state of rapid quantum fluctuations - from nothing.    Along with educating the reader on how the rigorous testing of evidence supports the theory that the universe sprang from nothing, Krauss finds it of equal importance to educate the reader by clarifying what \\"nothing\\" really is and how the scientific understanding or usage of the term \\"nothing\\" is more informative than the Philosophers or Theologians semantic, hopelessly abstract and non applicable definition.  Since the theologian contributes nothing more than mere backpedaling the definition into obscurity, Krauss argues that the scientific understanding of nothingness indeed replaces the relevatory or imaginative proclamations with more useful operational efforts to describe \\"nothingness\\".    Early on, Krauss sets the tone for the reader to explore implications by scientific discoveries by providing the scientific ethos for furthering our understanding of nature;  1) Follow evidence wherever it leads, even if the conclusions are startling or unexpected  2) If one has a theory, one needs to be willing to try to prove it wrong as much as one tries to prove it right  3) The ultimate arbiter of truth is experiment.    Throughout the chapters the reader is taken through the steps of the last 100 years in how the idea the ideas of size and shape of our Universe has drastically changed through a push and pull of theoretical science either being confirmed or denied by the advancement of scientific observation and testing.  This fascinating tale evokes many of the centuries observational discoveries in Physics and cosmology such as Edward Hubble, who confirmed that the expansion of the universe wasn't only happeningbut in fact accelerating, disproving Einsteins formulation of a \\"static universe\\" -  Other profound discoveries are thrown into consideration such as the \\"CMBR\\" ( Cosmic Microwave Background Radiation ) which supported the theory of the \\"big bang\\", or of the methods of how Krauss and others took in calculating the shape of the universe - leading to other profound discoveries such as the concept of \\"dark matter\\" ( or dark energy\\" ).  These discoveries in turn lead to other startling discoveries of our universe. . .which leads the path to the the thesis of the book.  I found the this section always engaging with Krauss always employs different methods, facetious analogies or graphs in helping the layman understand how the other scientists came to their conclusions, always underlining his useful premise;  \\"The Universe is the way it is whether we like it or not\\".    The integrity of Krauss work is further demonstrated with Krauss mentioning how he was cautious with the first data that comes in, might not be representative of all the data, systematic errors that could affect the data. \\"Speculations are fine, especially if it promotes new avenues for experiment. Krauss further calls for composure and being conservative as possible when examining real data.  In the last chapters the reader finds a wonderful description of processes of how a universe could come from nothing.   Some of the conditions that conspired to produce the shape of the universe that it is today such as the process of Inflation, whereby the energy of empty space/nothing gets converted into the energy of something - Empty space, endowed with enough stored energy which dominates the expansion of the universe, effectively creating everything we see.  It was mind-bending to discover that what we know or perceive as \\"empty space\\", is an unstable boiling brew of virtual particles that \\"pop\\" in and out of existence.  This fluctuation of energy happens so quickly that only the indirect effects of virtual particles can be measured before a positron meets the first electron, coincidentally annihilating each other and thereby returning back into empty space.    Krauss details more on the scientific method by citing the \\"paragon of good science\\" as when scientists use models that can be tested and falsified. Instead of working with questions of \\"why\\" something works, the work and inquiry of the scientist is to frame these into \\"how\\" questions, which allows the production of new knowledge and understanding of the phenomena. \\"How\\" questions are thus differentiated from purely theological questions, which presume the answers up front.  Richard Dawkins is also featured in the afterward, contributing a shorthanded yet chatty recap, successfully solidifying concepts that perhaps the reader was only beginning to correctly conceptualize together with underlining the significance of Krauss' work in the face of Philosophica and/or Theologian myopia . . .  In all, I thoroughly enjoyed Krauss' narrative and his facetious nature in trying to relate this profound and convincing discussion to the reader interested in Science, or those who follow the developments and discoveries more closely.

B000002U84|5.0|The first time I ever heard of Duran Duran was when some UHF channel ran a daily half-hour of cutting edge music videos (for 1981), and they constantly ran \\"Planet Earth.\\"  My niece and I used to laugh and I said, \\"You want to bet we never hear from these guys again?\\"  A year later, I was a senior in high school, and this song kept getting played on the radio, a funky little ditty with infectious basslines, murmurings about being \\"hungry like the wolf,\\" and somebody told me it was a new group called Duran Duran who did the song.  I was shell-shocked!  I wasn't entirely won over until I saw how well they performed on Saturday Night Live (Look for the installment with guest host Robert Guilluame), and rushed right out to buy Rio.  To say this album is a classic masterpiece from my youth is an understatement; contrary to the naysayers of the time, critics in particular, Rio has stood the test of time due to its outstanding quality, style, and downright addictively catchy new wave tinged dance pop.  These days a lot of the new wave artists have been reclassified as power pop, and Rio certainly fits into that mold exceptionally well.<br /><br />It is amazing that a group of five new romantics could take 70s rock influences such as David Bowie, Kraftwerk, their most obvious influence Avalon era Roxy Music, marry it with the disco and funk sensibilities of Chic, and come out with such an amazingly appealing sound.  Driven by John Taylor's phenomenally addictive bass lines, Nick Rhodes' superb skills on synthesizers, Roger Taylor's steady, strong drumwork, Andy Taylor's scorching guitar riffs, and Simon Le Bon's distinct vocals and poetry, there isn't worthy of dismissal here.  Set aside the fact that the \\"Fab Five\\" were considered throwaway pretty boys by the press and lots of Van Halen fans and really listen to this album.<br /><br />Not just stars of some of the best shot videos of the 80s, the band is brilliantly light and dark, innovative with their obvious influences, and this, their best album, is representative of true innovative talent.  You already know when you see that album cover designed by Patrick Nagel that their sophomore effort is something special, and from the moment \\"Rio,\\" a tribute to America--not a girl--you can't help really listening to the magical gelling of the groups components into one cohesive unit.  Like a well-oiled engine, the production by Colin Thurston is exceptionally clear and slick, and each track stands on its own as a veritable 9-track hit singles collection.  Consisting of disco and funk fueled rockers such as \\"My Own Way,\\" classic hit \\"Hungry Like the Wolf,\\" and \\"Hold Back the Rain,\\" then the catchy popisms of the title track and \\"Lonely in Your Nightmare,\\" there is already enough versatility to hold your attention.  Erotic, anthemic \\"Save a Prayer\\" featuring Nick Rhodes bender-enhanced synth riffs and Simon Le Bon's hopelessly moody romantic lyrics is undisputably classic.  \\"New Religion\\" is a funky, brooding social commentary, while \\"The Chauffeur\\" is a Hitchkock-ian piece that out-broods most any other number the group has ever recorded to date, complete with that plucky synth and those handclaps.  For me, by far and away, the best song on Rio is \\"Last Chance On the Stairway,\\" probably one of the best 80s pop songs I've ever had the pleasure of hearing.  Wonderful in every way, this is a tightly put together number without one component out of place.  What sends it over the top is Simon's dreamy, romantic lyric, a touch of marimba and, best of all, one of my favorite guitar solos of all time.  In fact, I think this is my favorite of all of Andy's solos.<br /><br />If you are a newcomer to Duran Duran and Rio in particular, congratulations and welcome from an old-timer!  You've just proven a lot of naysayers wrong in claiming they would never appeal years on down the line.  This is truly one of the best 80s releases you will ever lay down money for, and you've discovered for yourself what we knew all along.  Duran Duran weren't to be taken lightly, and they still aren't.  I hope you enjoy this album for years like so many of us here have, and pass it on.  There's no substitute for well-done music no matter what era it's from.weren't to be taken lightly, and they still aren't.  I hope you enjoy this album for years like so many of us here have, and pass it on.  There's no substitute for well-done music no matter what era it's from.

B002NZV68U|1.0|Let's face it: In life there is good-androgynous and bad androgynous. Pat in this case was a character of the most creepy androgyny, in an almost genius direction, and very funny. It highlighted what most feel, and certainly also gay people, that there  are differences in styles of androgyny. I am someone who likes masculinity as a cultural trope and biological ethos, and I see the virtue in it, which is not always recognized in our somewhat feminized culture. But at the same time, I am not bothered nor do I dislike fem guys, and just consider it a variation, and often a witty and charming  and beautiful one. But the  character made of the central Willy Wonka figure in this remake of the original Willy Wonka is just bad-androgynous in every way,  and very creepy to boot. Unlike the original with Gene Wilder character,  who was quite  delightfully oddball in his masculine way.  In fact one of the reasons I think the movie was such a failure is that it was  simply quite impossible to imagine reasonable parents  leaving children with this character. There were some good points in the movie, but since this central weirdness was a bit anxiety- causing, it failed as entertainment. Ironically, one of Johnny Depp's other movies actually proves the point exactly. When he played that pirate in many movies, it would be hard to conjure a simultaneously  more masculine yet swishy character. He swished through every scene, and was very fem in many ways. And yet, whatever that button is, he did not push it for creepiness. By contrast Wonka in this one surely does.<br /><br />This makes me think also how jejune and unprofitable it is  to read too much into these movies. He said Willy Wonka was like a Christ-figure and the Oompa-Loopmas were like the Apostles. He even said that when they break open that giant  colorful toadstool and it flows out with liquid chocolate, that such was like almost a Eucharistic sacrament!! Well, obviously the nun who taught the class was pretty annoyed by this analysis. The silliness of it reminds me how taking mere entertainment with all its ad hoc effluvia and factoids as descriptors of some larger meaning precludes our ability to plumb real lines of meaning, and find the actual compass by which to hermeneutically analyze the essential entertainment ethos. 0679736646|5.0|This was the second Philip K. Dick book I've read - I love his writing and I'm eager to read more. He uses common science fiction elements, such as telepaths and cryonic suspension, but his writing makes them fresh. In this case, regular people can use special devices to communicate to people in cryonic suspension at special facilities. The experience of these people in \\"half-life\\" is a completely different sort of world which people in the \\"real world\\" can interact with. Dick uses this plot device to raise questions about reality and drive some interesting plot twists. I enjoy his writing because it makes you think, whether it be about consumerism or what is real; it is very entertaining; and while it is an \\"easy read\\", it is still well-written. When the world around the characters begins to slide back into time gradually with prop planes replacing jetliners and antique cars appearing, Dick describes it so well it's easy to get lost in that time. While the ending can be somewhat puzzling, it fits the story well with the sense of unreality prevalent through much of it. The quotes about ubik at the start of every chapter were both amusing and puzzling. In fact, I would say that's a fair assessment of the book as whole - amusing and (deliberately) puzzling.
